<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Constitution</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Fundamental principles that govern a state</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Constitution_(disambiguation)" title="Constitution (disambiguation)">Constitution (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1102" data-file-width="741" decoding="async" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg/220px-Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg/330px-Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg/440px-Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constitution_de_l%27an_XII._Page_1_-_Archives_Nationales_-_AE-II-1512.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Year_XII" title="Constitution of the Year XII">Constitution of the Year XII</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_French_Republic" title="First French Republic">First French Republic</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg"><img alt="Constitution in 1848." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="1455" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg/220px-Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg/330px-Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg/440px-Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Costituzione_del_Regno_di_Napoli_del_1848.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Constitution of the Kingdom of Naples in 1848.</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>constitution</b> is the aggregate of fundamental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principles" title="Principles">principles</a> or established <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precedents" title="Precedents">precedents</a> that constitute the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal" title="Legal">legal</a> basis of a <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation" title="Organisation">organisation</a> or other type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_entity" title="Legal entity">entity</a> and commonly determine how that entity is to be governed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>When these principles are written down into a single document or set of legal documents, those documents may be said to embody a <i>written constitution</i>; if they are encompassed in a single comprehensive document, it is said to embody a <i>codified constitution</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Constitution_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="The Constitution of the United Kingdom">The Constitution of the United Kingdom</a> is a notable example of an <i>uncodified constitution</i>; it is instead written in numerous fundamental Acts of a legislature, court cases or treaties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Constitutions concern different levels of organizations, from <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign countries</a> to <a href="/wiki/Company" title="Company">companies</a> and unincorporated <a href="/wiki/Club_(organization)" title="Club (organization)">associations</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> which establishes an <a href="/wiki/International_organization" title="International organization">international organization</a> is also its constitution, in that it would define how that organization is constituted. Within <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">states</a>, a constitution defines the principles upon which the state is based, the procedure in which laws are made and by whom. Some constitutions, especially codified constitutions, also act as limiters of state power, by establishing lines which a state's rulers cannot cross, such as <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_rights" title="Fundamental rights">fundamental rights</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Constitution of India</a> is the longest written constitution of any country in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-longest_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-longest-3">[3]</a></sup> with 146,385 words<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> in its <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">English-language</a> version,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawmin_info_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-lawmin_info-5">[5]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Monaco" title="Constitution of Monaco">Constitution of Monaco</a> is the shortest written constitution with 3,814 words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_San_Marino" title="Constitution of San Marino">Constitution of San Marino</a> might be the world's oldest active written constitution, since some of its core documents have been in operation since 1600, while the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution of the United States</a> is the oldest active codified constitution. The historical life expectancy of a constitution since 1789 is approximately 19 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The term <i>constitution</i> comes through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_(language)" title="French (language)">French</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> word <i>constitutio</i>, used for regulations and orders, such as the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial</a> enactments (<i>constitutiones principis</i>: edicta, mandata, decreta, rescripta).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Later, the term was widely used in <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> for an important determination, especially a decree issued by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a>, now referred to as an <i><a href="/wiki/Apostolic_constitution" title="Apostolic constitution">apostolic constitution</a></i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a> used the term for significant and egregious violations of public trust, of a nature and extent that the transgression would justify a <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary" title="Revolutionary">revolutionary</a> response. The term as used by Blackstone was not for a legal text, nor did he intend to include the later American concept of <a href="/wiki/Judicial_review" title="Judicial review">judicial review</a>: "for that were to set the judicial power above that of the legislature, which would be subversive of all government".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_features">General features</span></h2>
<p>Generally, every modern written constitution confers specific powers on an organization or institutional entity, established upon the primary condition that it abides by the constitution's limitations. According to Scott Gordon, a political organization is constitutional to the extent that it "contain[s] <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutionalized</a> mechanisms of power control for the protection of the interests and <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberties</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizenry" title="Citizenry">citizenry</a>, including those that may be in the <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">minority</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon1999_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon1999-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Activities of officials within an organization or polity that fall within the constitutional or statutory authority of those officials are termed "within power" (or, in Latin, <i>intra vires</i>); if they do not, they are termed "beyond power" (or, in Latin, <i><a href="/wiki/Ultra_vires" title="Ultra vires">ultra vires</a></i>). For example, a <a href="/wiki/Students%27_union" title="Students' union">students' union</a> may be prohibited as an organization from engaging in activities not concerning students; if the union becomes involved in non-student activities, these activities are considered to be <i>ultra vires</i> of the union's charter, and nobody would be compelled by the charter to follow them. An example from the constitutional law of <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a> would be a provincial <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliament</a> in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_state" title="Federal state">federal state</a> trying to legislate in an area that the constitution allocates exclusively to the federal parliament, such as ratifying a treaty.  Action that appears to be beyond power may be <a href="/wiki/Judicial_review" title="Judicial review">judicially reviewed</a> and, if found to be beyond power, must cease. Legislation that is found to be beyond power will be "invalid" and of no force; this applies to primary legislation, requiring constitutional authorization, and secondary legislation, ordinarily requiring statutory authorization. In this context, "within power", <i>intra vires</i>, "authorized" and "valid" have the same meaning; as do "beyond power", <i>ultra vires</i>, "not authorized" and "invalid".
</p><p>In most but not all modern states the constitution has supremacy over ordinary <a href="/wiki/Statutory_law" title="Statutory law">statutory law</a> (see <a href="#Uncodified_constitution">Uncodified constitution</a> below); in such states when an official act is unconstitutional, i.e. it is not a power granted to the government by the constitution, that act is <i>null and void</i>, and the nullification is <i><a href="/wiki/Ab_initio" title="Ab initio">ab initio</a></i>, that is, from inception, not from the date of the finding. It was never "law", even though, if it had been a statute or statutory provision, it might have been adopted according to the procedures for adopting legislation. Sometimes the problem is not that a statute is unconstitutional, but that the application of it is, on a particular occasion, and a court may decide that while there are ways it could be applied that are constitutional, that instance was not allowed or legitimate. In such a case, only that application may be ruled unconstitutional. Historically, the remedies for such violations have been petitions for common law <a href="/wiki/Writ" title="Writ">writs</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Quo_warranto" title="Quo warranto">quo warranto</a></i>.
</p><p>Scholars debate whether a constitution must necessarily be <a href="/wiki/Autochthon_(ancient_Greece)" title="Autochthon (ancient Greece)">autochthonous</a>, resulting from the nations "spirit". <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a> said "A constitution...is the work of centuries; it is the idea, the consciousness of rationality so far as that consciousness is developed in a particular nation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_development">History and development</span></h2>
<p>Since 1789, along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Constitution of the United States of America">Constitution of the United States of America</a> (U.S. Constitution), which is the oldest and shortest written constitution still in force,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> close to 800 constitutions have been adopted and subsequently amended  around the world by independent states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> predicted that a period of 20 years would be the optimal time for any constitution to be still in force, since "the earth belongs to the living, and not to the dead."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Indeed, according to recent studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-13">[13]</a></sup> the average life of any new written constitution is around 19 years. However, a great number of constitutions do not last more than 10 years, and around 10% do not last more than one year, as was the case of the <a href="/wiki/French_Constitution_of_1791" title="French Constitution of 1791">French Constitution of 1791</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-13">[13]</a></sup> By contrast, some constitutions, notably that of the United States, have remained in force for several centuries, often without major revision for long periods of time.
</p><p>The most common reasons for these frequent changes are the political desire for an immediate outcome<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the short time devoted to the constitutional drafting process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-15">[15]</a></sup> A study in 2009 showed that the average time taken to draft a constitution is around 16 months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-16">[16]</a></sup>  however there were also some extreme cases registered. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> 2008 Constitution was being secretly drafted for more than 17 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-16">[16]</a></sup> whereas at the other extreme, during the drafting of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>'s 1946 Constitution, the bureaucrats drafted everything in no more than a week. Japan has the oldest unamended constitution in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anomalous-constitution_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-anomalous-constitution-17">[17]</a></sup> The record for the shortest overall process of drafting, adoption, and ratification of a national constitution belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>'s 1938 constitution, which installed a royal dictatorship in less than a month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Studies showed that typically extreme cases where the constitution-making process either takes too long or is extremely short were non-democracies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Constitutional rights are not a specific characteristic of democratic countries. Non-democratic countries have constitutions, such as that of <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, which officially grants every citizen, among other rights, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" title="Freedom of expression">freedom of expression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-modern_constitutions">Pre-modern constitutions</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient">Ancient</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hammurabi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hammurabi.jpg/170px-Hammurabi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hammurabi.jpg/255px-Hammurabi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hammurabi.jpg/340px-Hammurabi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hammurabi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail from <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">Hammurabi</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> shows him receiving the laws of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> from the seated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_deity" title="Sun deity">sun deity</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Excavations in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_de_Sarzec" title="Ernest de Sarzec">Ernest de Sarzec</a> in 1877 found evidence of the earliest known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_of_justice" title="Code of justice">code of justice</a>, issued by the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Urukagina" title="Urukagina">Urukagina</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a> c. 2300 BC. Perhaps the earliest prototype for a law of government, this document itself has not yet been discovered; however it is known that it allowed some rights to his citizens. For example, it is known that it relieved tax for widows and orphans, and protected the poor from the <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a> of the rich.
</p><p>After that, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forms_of_government" title="Forms of government">governments</a> ruled by special codes of written laws. The oldest such document still known to exist seems to be the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Ur-Nammu" title="Code of Ur-Nammu">Code of Ur-Nammu</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a> (c. 2050 BC). Some of the better-known ancient law codes are the code of <a href="/wiki/Lipit-Ishtar" title="Lipit-Ishtar">Lipit-Ishtar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">Isin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">code of Hammurabi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_laws" title="Hittite laws">Hittite code</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_law" title="Assyrian law">Assyrian code</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/613_Commandments" title="613 Commandments">Mosaic law</a>.
</p><p>In 621 BC, a scribe named <a href="/wiki/Draco_(lawgiver)" title="Draco (lawgiver)">Draco</a> codified the oral laws of the <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>; this code prescribed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_penalty" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a> for many offenses (thus creating the modern term "draconian" for very strict rules). In 594 BC, <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a>, the ruler of Athens, created the new <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solonian_Constitution" title="Solonian Constitution">Solonian Constitution</a></i>. It eased the burden of the workers, and determined that membership of the ruling class was to be based on wealth (<a href="/wiki/Plutocracy" title="Plutocracy">plutocracy</a>), rather than on birth (<a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleisthenes_of_Athens" title="Cleisthenes of Athens">Cleisthenes</a> again reformed the Athenian constitution and set it on a democratic footing in 508 BC.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="608" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png/300px-Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png/450px-Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png/600px-Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_constitutions_diagram.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram illustrating the classification of constitutions by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (c. 350 BC) was the first to make a formal distinction between ordinary law and constitutional law, establishing ideas of constitution and <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">constitutionalism</a>, and attempting to classify different forms of constitutional government. The most basic definition he used to describe a constitution in general terms was "the arrangement of the offices in a state". In his works <i><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Athenians_(Aristotle)" title="Constitution of the Athenians (Aristotle)">Constitution of Athens</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>, he explores different constitutions of his day, including those of Athens, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycurgus_of_Sparta" title="Lycurgus of Sparta">Sparta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>. He classified both what he regarded as good and what he regarded as bad constitutions, and came to the conclusion that the best constitution was a mixed system including monarchic, aristocratic, and democratic elements. He also distinguished between citizens, who had the right to participate in the state, and non-citizens and slaves, who did not.
</p><p>The Romans initially codified their constitution in 450 BC as the <i><a href="/wiki/Twelve_Tables" title="Twelve Tables">Twelve Tables</a></i>. They operated under a series of laws that were added from time to time, but <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> was not reorganised into a single code until the <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Theodosianus" title="Codex Theodosianus">Codex Theodosianus</a></i> (438 AD); later, in the Eastern Empire, the <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Codex repetitæ prælectionis</a></i> (534) was highly influential throughout Europe. This was followed in the east by the <i>Ecloga</i> of <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III the Isaurian</a> (740) and the <i>Basilica</i> of <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a> (878).
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a></i> established constitutional principles for the 3rd century BC <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya</a> king's rule in <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">India</a>. For constitutional principles almost lost to antiquity, see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_of_Manu" title="Code of Manu">code of Manu</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span></h4>
<p>Many of the Germanic peoples that filled the power vacuum left by the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> codified their laws. One of the first of these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_tribal_laws" title="Germanic tribal laws">Germanic law codes</a> to be written was the Visigothic <i>Code of <a href="/wiki/Euric" title="Euric">Euric</a></i> (471 AD). This was followed by the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Burgundionum" title="Lex Burgundionum">Lex Burgundionum</a></i>, applying separate codes for Germans and for Romans; the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Alamannorum" title="Lex Alamannorum">Pactus Alamannorum</a></i>; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salic_Law" title="Salic Law">Salic Law</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, all written soon after 500. In 506, the <i><a href="/wiki/Breviary_of_Alaric" title="Breviary of Alaric">Breviarum</a></i> or <i>"Lex Romana"</i> of <a href="/wiki/Alaric_II" title="Alaric II">Alaric II</a>, king of the Visigoths, adopted and consolidated the <i>Codex Theodosianus</i> together with assorted earlier Roman laws. Systems that appeared somewhat later include the <i><a href="/wiki/Edictum_Rothari" title="Edictum Rothari">Edictum Rothari</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> (643), the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lex_Visigothorum" title="Lex Visigothorum">Lex Visigothorum</a></i> (654), the <i>Lex Alamannorum</i> (730), and the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Frisionum" title="Lex Frisionum">Lex Frisionum</a></i> (c. 785). These continental codes were all composed in Latin, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_language" title="Anglo-Saxon language">Anglo-Saxon</a> was used for those of England, beginning with the Code of <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelberht_of_Kent" title="Æthelberht of Kent">Æthelberht of Kent</a> (602). Around 893, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a> combined this and two other earlier Saxon codes, with various Mosaic and Christian precepts, to produce the <i><a href="/wiki/Doom_book" title="Doom book">Doom book</a></i> code of laws for England.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Seventeen-article_constitution" title="Seventeen-article constitution">Seventeen-article constitution</a></i> written in 604, reportedly by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prince_Shotoku" title="Prince Shotoku">Prince Shōtoku</a>, is an early example of a constitution in Asian political history. Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> teachings, the document focuses more on social morality than on institutions of government, and remains a notable early attempt at a government constitution.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Medina" title="Constitution of Medina">Constitution of Medina</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">صحیفة المدینه</span>, Ṣaḥīfat al-Madīna), also known as the Charter of Medina, was drafted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_prophet" title="Islamic prophet">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> after his flight (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">hijra</a>) to Yathrib where he became political leader. It constituted a formal agreement between Muhammad and all of the significant tribes and families of Yathrib (later known as <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>), including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jews</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The document was drawn up with the explicit concern of bringing to an end the bitter intertribal fighting between the clans of the Aws (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banu_Aus" title="Banu Aus">Aus</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khazraj" title="Khazraj">Khazraj</a> within Medina. To this effect it instituted a number of rights and responsibilities for the Muslim, Jewish, and pagan communities of Medina bringing them within the fold of one community – the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The precise dating of the Constitution of Medina remains debated, but generally scholars agree it was written shortly after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a> (622).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Welsh_law" title="Welsh law">Cyfraith Hywel</a></i> (Law of Hywel) was codified by <a href="/wiki/Hywel_Dda" title="Hywel Dda">Hywel Dda</a> c. 942–950.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages_after_1000">Middle Ages after 1000</span></h4>
<p>The <i>Pravda Yaroslava</i>, originally combined by <a href="/wiki/Yaroslav_the_Wise" title="Yaroslav the Wise">Yaroslav the Wise</a> the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Ukrainian_rulers" title="List of Ukrainian rulers">Grand Prince of Kyiv</a>, was granted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Novgorod" title="Great Novgorod">Great Novgorod</a> around 1017, and in 1054 was incorporated into the <i><a href="/wiki/Russkaya_Pravda" title="Russkaya Pravda">Ruska Pravda</a></i>; it became the law for all of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus" title="Kievan Rus">Kievan Rus</a>. It survived only in later editions of the 15th century.
</p><p>In England, <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I's</a> proclamation of the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Liberties" title="Charter of Liberties">Charter of Liberties</a> in 1100 bound the king for the first time in his treatment of the clergy and the nobility. This idea was extended and refined by the English barony when they forced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_England" title="John of England">King John</a> to sign <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i> in 1215. The most important single article of the <i>Magna Carta</i>, related to "<i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i>", provided that the king was not permitted to imprison, outlaw, exile or kill anyone at a whim – there must be <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a> of law first. This article, Article 39, of the <i>Magna Carta</i> read:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No free man shall be arrested, or imprisoned, or deprived of his property, or outlawed, or exiled, or in any way destroyed, nor shall we go against him or send against him, unless by legal judgement of his peers, or by the law of the land.</p></blockquote>
<p>This provision became the cornerstone of English liberty after that point. The <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> in the original case was between the king and the nobility, but was gradually extended to all of the people. It led to the system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_Monarchy" title="Constitutional Monarchy">Constitutional Monarchy</a>, with further reforms shifting the balance of power from the monarchy and nobility to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Nomocanon" title="Nomocanon">Nomocanon</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Sava" title="Saint Sava">Saint Sava</a> (<a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a>: <span lang="sr">Законоправило/Zakonopravilo</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> was the first <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbian</a> constitution from 1219. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Sava%27s_Nomocanon" title="St. Sava's Nomocanon">St. Sava's Nomocanon</a> was the compilation of <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a>, based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Law" title="Roman Law">Roman Law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a>, based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Councils" title="Ecumenical Councils">Ecumenical Councils</a>. Its basic purpose was to organize the functioning of the young <a href="/wiki/Serbia_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Serbia in the Middle Ages">Serbian kingdom</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serbian_Ortodox_Church" title="Serbian Ortodox Church">Serbian church</a>. Saint Sava began the work on the Serbian Nomocanon in 1208 while he was at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Athos" title="Mount Athos">Mount Athos</a>, using <i>The Nomocanon in Fourteen Titles</i>, <i>Synopsis of Stefan the Efesian</i>, <i>Nomocanon of <a href="/wiki/John_Scholasticus" title="John Scholasticus">John Scholasticus</a></i>, and Ecumenical Council documents, which he modified with the canonical commentaries of Aristinos and <a href="/wiki/Joannes_Zonaras" title="Joannes Zonaras">Joannes Zonaras</a>, local church meetings, rules of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Fathers" title="Holy Fathers">Holy Fathers</a>, the law of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, the translation of Prohiron, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperors" title="Byzantine emperors">Byzantine emperors</a>' <a href="/wiki/Novellae_Constitutiones" title="Novellae Constitutiones">Novellae</a> (most were taken from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a>'s Novellae). The Nomocanon was a completely new compilation of civil and canonical regulations, taken from <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> sources but completed and reformed by St. Sava to function properly in Serbia. Besides decrees that organized the life of church, there are various norms regarding civil life; most of these were taken from Prohiron. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_transplants" title="Legal transplants">Legal transplants</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Law" title="Roman Law">Roman</a>-<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_law" title="Byzantine law">Byzantine law</a> became the basis of the Serbian medieval law. The essence of Zakonopravilo was based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corpus_Iuris_Civilis" title="Corpus Iuris Civilis">Corpus Iuris Civilis</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stefan_Du%C5%A1an" title="Stefan Dušan">Stefan Dušan</a>, emperor of Serbs and Greeks, enacted <a href="/wiki/Du%C5%A1an%27s_Code" title="Dušan's Code">Dušan's Code</a> (<a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a>: <span lang="sr">Душанов Законик/Dušanov Zakonik</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, in two state congresses: in 1349 in <a href="/wiki/Skopje" title="Skopje">Skopje</a> and in 1354 in <a href="/wiki/Serres" title="Serres">Serres</a>. It regulated all social spheres, so it was the second Serbian constitution, after St. Sava's Nomocanon (Zakonopravilo). The Code was based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Law" title="Roman Law">Roman</a>-<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_law" title="Byzantine law">Byzantine law</a>. The legal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_transplants" title="Legal transplants">transplanting</a> within articles 171 and 172 of Dušan's Code, which regulated the juridical independence, is notable. They were taken from the Byzantine code <a href="/wiki/Basilika" title="Basilika">Basilika</a> (book VII, 1, 16–17).
</p><p>In 1222, Hungarian King <a href="/wiki/Andrew_II_of_Hungary" title="Andrew II of Hungary">Andrew II</a> issued the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull_of_1222" title="Golden Bull of 1222">Golden Bull of 1222</a>.
</p><p>Between 1220 and 1230, a <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxon</a> administrator, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eike_von_Repgow" title="Eike von Repgow">Eike von Repgow</a>, composed the <i><a href="/wiki/Sachsenspiegel" title="Sachsenspiegel">Sachsenspiegel</a></i>, which became the supreme law used in parts of Germany as late as 1900.
</p><p>Around 1240, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copt" title="Copt">Coptic</a> Egyptian Christian writer, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%27Abul_Fada%27il_Ibn_al-%27Assal&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="'Abul Fada'il Ibn al-'Assal (page does not exist)">'Abul Fada'il Ibn al-'Assal</a>, wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Fetha_Negest" title="Fetha Negest">Fetha Negest</a></i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>. 'Ibn al-Assal took his laws partly from apostolic writings and Mosaic law and partly from the former <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> codes. There are a few historical records claiming that this law code was translated into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a> and entered Ethiopia around 1450 in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Zara_Yaqob" title="Zara Yaqob">Zara Yaqob</a>. Even so, its first recorded use in the function of a constitution (supreme law of the land) is with <a href="/wiki/Sarsa_Dengel" title="Sarsa Dengel">Sarsa Dengel</a> beginning in 1563. The <i>Fetha Negest</i> remained the supreme law in Ethiopia until 1931, when a modern-style <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ethiopia" title="Constitution of Ethiopia">Constitution</a> was first granted by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie" title="Haile Selassie">Haile Selassie</a> I.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConstCATMonso1535.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2918" data-file-width="2060" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/ConstCATMonso1535.png/170px-ConstCATMonso1535.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/ConstCATMonso1535.png/255px-ConstCATMonso1535.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/ConstCATMonso1535.png/340px-ConstCATMonso1535.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ConstCATMonso1535.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Third volume of the compilation of Catalan Constitutions of 1585</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Catalonia" title="Principality of Catalonia">Principality of Catalonia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Catalan_constitutions" title="Catalan constitutions">Catalan constitutions</a> were promulgated by the Court from 1283 (or even two centuries before, if  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Usatges_of_Barcelona" title="Usatges of Barcelona">Usatges of Barcelona</a> is considered part of the compilation of Constitutions) until 1716, when <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Spain" title="Philip V of Spain">Philip V of Spain</a> gave the <a href="/wiki/Nueva_Planta_decrees" title="Nueva Planta decrees">Nueva Planta decrees</a>, finishing with the historical laws of <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>. These Constitutions were usually made formally as a royal initiative, but required for its approval or repeal the favorable vote of the <a href="/wiki/Catalan_Courts" title="Catalan Courts">Catalan Courts</a>, the medieval antecedent of the modern Parliaments. These laws, like other modern constitutions, had preeminence over other laws, and they could not be contradicted by mere decrees or edicts of the king.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kouroukan_Fouga" title="Kouroukan Fouga">Kouroukan Founga</a> was a 13th-century charter of the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a>, reconstructed from oral tradition in 1988 by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Siriman_Kouyat%C3%A9&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Siriman Kouyaté (page does not exist)">Siriman Kouyaté</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull_of_1356" title="Golden Bull of 1356">Golden Bull of 1356</a> was a decree issued by a <i><a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Reichstag</a></i> in Nuremberg headed by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a> that fixed, for a period of more than four hundred years, an important aspect of the constitutional structure of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> created and refined a document he called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zu_Xun" title="Huang Ming Zu Xun">Ancestral Injunctions</a></i> (first published in 1375, revised twice more before his death in 1398). These rules served as a constitution for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> for the next 250 years.
</p><p>The oldest written document still governing a sovereign nation today is that of <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_San_Marino" title="Constitution of San Marino">Leges Statutae Republicae Sancti Marini</a></i> was written in Latin and consists of six books. The first book, with 62 articles, establishes councils, courts, various executive officers, and the powers assigned to them. The remaining books cover criminal and civil law and judicial procedures and remedies. Written in 1600, the document was based upon the <i>Statuti Comunali</i> (Town Statute) of 1300, itself influenced by the <i>Codex Justinianus</i>, and it remains in force today.
</p><p>In 1392 the <i><a href="/wiki/Carta_de_Logu" title="Carta de Logu">Carta de Logu</a></i> was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_code" title="Legal code">legal code</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giudicato_of_Arborea" title="Giudicato of Arborea">Giudicato of Arborea</a> promulgated by the <i>giudicessa</i> <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Arborea" title="Eleanor of Arborea">Eleanor</a>. It was in force in <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> until it was superseded by the code of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Felix_of_Sardinia" title="Charles Felix of Sardinia">Charles Felix</a> in April 1827. The Carta was a work of great importance in <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinian</a> history. It was an organic, coherent, and systematic work of legislation encompassing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_law_(area)" title="Civil law (area)">civil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penal_law" title="Penal law">penal law</a>.
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Law_of_Peace" title="Great Law of Peace">Gayanashagowa</a></i>, the oral constitution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haudenosaunee" title="Haudenosaunee">Haudenosaunee</a> nation also known as the Great Law of Peace, established a system of governance as far back as 1190 AD (though perhaps more recently at 1451) in which the <a href="/wiki/Sachem" title="Sachem">Sachems</a>, or tribal chiefs, of the Iroquois League's member nations made decisions on the basis of universal consensus of all chiefs following discussions that were initiated by a single nation. The position of Sachem descends through families and are allocated by the senior female clan heads, though, prior to the filling of the position, candidacy is ultimately democratically decided by the community itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tooker_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tooker-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_constitutions">Modern constitutions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5168" data-file-width="3568" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Filip_Orliks_konstitution_front_1-crop.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cossack <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Pylyp_Orlyk" title="Constitution of Pylyp Orlyk">Constitution of Pylyp Orlyk</a>, 1710.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg/300px-Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg/450px-Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg/600px-Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Washington_Constitutional_Convention_1787.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting depicting George Washington at the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a> of 1787 signing of the U.S. Constitution</div></div></div>
<p>In 1634 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sweden" title="Kingdom of Sweden">Kingdom of Sweden</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government_(1634)" title="Instrument of Government (1634)">1634 Instrument of Government</a>, drawn up under the <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Chancellor_of_Sweden" title="Lord High Chancellor of Sweden">Lord High Chancellor of Sweden</a> <a href="/wiki/Axel_Oxenstierna" title="Axel Oxenstierna">Axel Oxenstierna</a> after the death of king <a href="/wiki/Gustavus_Adolphus" title="Gustavus Adolphus">Gustavus Adolphus</a>, it can be seen as the first written constitution adopted by a modern state.
</p><p>In 1639, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colony_of_Connecticut" title="Colony of Connecticut">Colony of Connecticut</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Orders_of_Connecticut" title="Fundamental Orders of Connecticut">Fundamental Orders</a>, which was the first <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> constitution, and is the basis for every new Connecticut constitution since, and is also the reason for <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>'s nickname, "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_State" title="Constitution State">Constitution State</a>".
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Protectorate" title="English Protectorate">English Protectorate</a> that was set up by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> after the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> promulgated the first detailed written constitution adopted by a modern state;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> it was called the <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government" title="Instrument of Government">Instrument of Government</a>. This formed the basis of government for the short-lived republic from 1653 to 1657 by providing a legal rationale for the increasing power of Cromwell after Parliament consistently failed to govern effectively. Most of the concepts and ideas embedded into modern constitutional theory, especially <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameralism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a>, the written constitution, and <a href="/wiki/Judicial_review" title="Judicial review">judicial review</a>, can be traced back to the experiments of that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Drafted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Lambert_(General)" title="John Lambert (General)">Major-General John Lambert</a> in 1653, the <i>Instrument of Government</i> included elements incorporated from an earlier document "<a href="/wiki/Heads_of_Proposals" title="Heads of Proposals">Heads of Proposals</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> which had been agreed to by the <a href="/wiki/Army_Council_(1647)" title="Army Council (1647)">Army Council</a> in 1647, as a set of propositions intended to be a basis for a constitutional settlement after King <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> was defeated in the <a href="/wiki/First_English_Civil_War" title="First English Civil War">First English Civil War</a>. Charles had rejected the propositions, but before the start of the Second Civil War, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grandee_(New_Model_Army)" title="Grandee (New Model Army)">Grandees</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_Model_Army" title="New Model Army">New Model Army</a> had presented the <i>Heads of Proposals</i> as their alternative to the more radical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agreement_of_the_People" title="Agreement of the People">Agreement of the People</a> presented by the Agitators and their civilian supporters at the <a href="/wiki/Putney_Debates" title="Putney Debates">Putney Debates</a>.
</p><p>On January 4, 1649, the <a href="/wiki/Rump_Parliament" title="Rump Parliament">Rump Parliament</a> declared "that the people are, under God, the original of all just power; that the Commons of England, being chosen by and representing the people, have the supreme power in this nation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritze_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fritze-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Instrument of Government</i> was adopted by Parliament on December 15, 1653, and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> was installed as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Protector" title="Lord Protector">Lord Protector</a> on the following day. The constitution set up a state council consisting of 21 members while executive authority was vested in the office of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Protector_of_the_Commonwealth" title="Lord Protector of the Commonwealth">Lord Protector of the Commonwealth</a>." This position was designated as a non-hereditary life appointment. The <i>Instrument</i> also required the calling of triennial <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">Parliaments</a>, with each sitting for at least five months.
</p><p>The <i>Instrument of Government</i> was replaced in May 1657 by England's second, and last, codified constitution, the <a href="/wiki/Humble_Petition_and_Advice" title="Humble Petition and Advice">Humble Petition and Advice</a>, proposed by Sir <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Packe_(politician)" title="Christopher Packe (politician)">Christopher Packe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The Petition offered hereditary <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, asserted <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a>'s control over issuing new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxation" title="Taxation">taxation</a>, provided an independent council to advise the king and safeguarded "Triennial" meetings of Parliament. A modified version of the Humble Petition with the clause on kingship removed was ratified on 25 May. This finally met its demise in conjunction with the death of Cromwell and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">Restoration</a> of the monarchy.
</p><p>Other examples of European constitutions of this era were the <a href="/wiki/Corsican_Constitution" title="Corsican Constitution">Corsican Constitution</a> of 1755 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swedish_Constitution_of_1772" title="Swedish Constitution of 1772">Swedish Constitution of 1772</a>.
</p><p>All of the British colonies in North America that were to become the 13 original United States, adopted their own constitutions in 1776 and 1777, during the American Revolution (and before the later <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>), with the exceptions of Massachusetts, Connecticut and Rhode Island. The <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Commonwealth of Massachusetts</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Massachusetts" title="Constitution of Massachusetts">its Constitution</a> in 1780, the oldest still-functioning constitution of any U.S. state; while Connecticut and Rhode Island officially continued to operate under their old colonial charters, until they adopted their first state constitutions in 1818 and 1843, respectively.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_constitutions">Democratic constitutions</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4135" data-file-width="7459" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg/330px-Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg/495px-Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg/660px-Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Konstytucja_3_Maja.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_May_3,_1791_(painting)" title="Constitution of May 3, 1791 (painting)">Constitution of May 3, 1791</a></i> (painting by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Matejko" title="Jan Matejko">Jan Matejko</a>, 1891). <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Polish</a> <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_August_Poniatowski" title="Stanisław August Poniatowski">King Stanisław August</a> (left, in regal <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">ermine</a>-trimmed cloak), enters <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._John%27s_Cathedral,_Warsaw" title="St. John's Cathedral, Warsaw">St. John's Cathedral</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Sejm" title="Sejm">Sejm</a> <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Deputies" title="Chamber of Deputies">deputies</a> will swear to uphold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_May_3,_1791" title="Constitution of May 3, 1791">the new Constitution</a>; in background, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warsaw%27s_Royal_Castle" title="Warsaw's Royal Castle">Warsaw's Royal Castle</a>, where the Constitution has just been adopted.</div></div></div>
<p>What is sometimes called the "enlightened constitution" model was developed by philosophers of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>. The model proposed that constitutional governments should be stable, adaptable, accountable, open and should represent the people (i.e., support <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Pylyp_Orlyk" title="Constitution of Pylyp Orlyk">Agreements and Constitutions of Laws and Freedoms of the Zaporizian Host</a></i> was written in 1710 by <a href="/wiki/Pylyp_Orlyk" title="Pylyp Orlyk">Pylyp Orlyk</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hetman" title="Hetman">hetman</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Zaporozhian_Host" title="Zaporozhian Host">Zaporozhian Host</a>. It was written to establish a free <a href="/wiki/Cossack_Hetmanate" title="Cossack Hetmanate">Zaporozhian-Ukrainian Republic</a>, with the support of <a href="/wiki/Charles_XII_of_Sweden" title="Charles XII of Sweden">Charles XII of Sweden</a>. It is notable in that it established a democratic standard for the separation of powers in government between the legislative, executive, and judiciary branches, well before the publication of Montesquieu's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_of_the_Laws" title="Spirit of the Laws">Spirit of the Laws</a></i>. This Constitution also limited the executive authority of the <i>hetman</i>, and established a democratically elected Cossack parliament called the General Council. However, Orlyk's project for an independent <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukrainian</a> State never materialized, and his constitution, written in exile, never went into effect.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Corsican_Constitution" title="Corsican Constitution">Corsican Constitutions</a> of 1755 and 1794 were inspired by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>. The latter introduced <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> for property owners.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government_(1772)" title="Instrument of Government (1772)">Swedish constitution of 1772</a> was enacted under King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gustavus_III" title="Gustavus III">Gustavus III</a> and was inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> by <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>. The king also cherished other <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">enlightenment</a> ideas (as an <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_absolutism" title="Enlightened absolutism">enlighted despot</a>) and repealed torture, liberated agricultural trade, diminished the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_penalty" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a> and instituted a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_freedom" title="Religious freedom">religious freedom</a>. The constitution was commended by <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borg_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borg-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-B-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borg412_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borg412-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>, ratified June 21, 1788, was influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">Locke</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_de_Secondat,_Baron_de_Montesquieu" title="Charles de Secondat, Baron de Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, and others. The document became a benchmark for <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> and codified constitutions written thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_May_3,_1791" title="Constitution of May 3, 1791">Constitution</a> was passed on May 3, 1791.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Markoff_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Markoff-45">[45]</a></sup> Its draft was developed by the leading minds of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Poland" title="Enlightenment in Poland">Enlightenment in Poland</a> such as King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stanislaw_August_Poniatowski" title="Stanislaw August Poniatowski">Stanislaw August Poniatowski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Staszic" title="Stanisław Staszic">Stanisław Staszic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scipione_Piattoli" title="Scipione Piattoli">Scipione Piattoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Ursyn_Niemcewicz" title="Julian Ursyn Niemcewicz">Julian Ursyn Niemcewicz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_Potocki" title="Ignacy Potocki">Ignacy Potocki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ko%C5%82%C5%82%C4%85taj" title="Hugo Kołłątaj">Hugo Kołłątaj</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> It was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Sejm" title="Great Sejm">Great Sejm</a> and is considered the first constitution of its kind in Europe and the world's second oldest one after the American Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another landmark document was the <a href="/wiki/French_Constitution_of_1791" title="French Constitution of 1791">French Constitution of 1791</a>.
</p><p>The 1811 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Venezuela" title="Constitution of Venezuela">Constitution of Venezuela</a> was the first Constitution of <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> and Latin America, promulgated and drafted by <a href="/wiki/Crist%C3%B3bal_Mendoza" title="Cristóbal Mendoza">Cristóbal Mendoza</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diccionario_Perozo_1999_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diccionario_Perozo_1999-48">[48]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Juan_Germ%C3%A1n_Roscio" title="Juan Germán Roscio">Juan Germán Roscio</a> and  in <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>. It established a federal government but was repealed one year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>On March 19, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Constitution_of_1812" title="Spanish Constitution of 1812">Spanish Constitution of 1812</a> was ratified by a <a href="/wiki/Cortes_Generales" title="Cortes Generales">parliament</a> gathered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiz" title="Cadiz">Cadiz</a>, the only Spanish continental city which was safe from <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">French occupation</a>. The Spanish Constitution served as a model for other liberal constitutions of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Europe" title="South Europe">South European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> nations, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Revolution_of_1820" title="Liberal Revolution of 1820">Portuguese Constitution of 1822</a>, constitutions of various <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian</a> states during <a href="/wiki/Carbonari" title="Carbonari">Carbonari</a> revolts (i.e., in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Two_Sicilies" title="Kingdom of the Two Sicilies">Kingdom of the Two Sicilies</a>), <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">the Norwegian constitution of 1814</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/1824_Constitution_of_Mexico" title="1824 Constitution of Mexico">Mexican Constitution of 1824</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Constitution_of_1824" title="Brazilian Constitution of 1824">Constitution of 1824</a> expressed the option for the monarchy as political system after Brazilian Independence. The leader of the national emancipation process was the Portuguese prince <a href="/wiki/Pedro_I_of_Brazil" title="Pedro I of Brazil">Pedro I</a>, elder son of the king of Portugal. Pedro was crowned in 1822 as first emperor of Brazil. The country was ruled by Constitutional monarchy until 1889, when it adopted the Republican model.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> lost its personal possession of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>. Sweden had already enacted its <a href="/wiki/Instrument_of_Government_(1809)" title="Instrument of Government (1809)">1809 Instrument of Government</a>, which saw the division of power between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riksdag_of_Sweden" title="Riksdag of Sweden">Riksdag</a>, the king and the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> However the Norwegians managed to infuse a radically democratic and liberal <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">constitution</a> in 1814, adopting many facets from the American constitution and the revolutionary French ones, but maintaining a hereditary <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">monarch</a> limited by the constitution, like the Spanish one.
</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Federal_Constitution" title="Swiss Federal Constitution">Swiss Federal Constitution</a> was put in force in September 1848 (with official revisions in 1878, 1891, 1949, 1971, 1982 and 1999).
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serbian_revolution" title="Serbian revolution">Serbian revolution</a> initially led to a proclamation of a proto-constitution in 1811; the full-fledged Constitution of Serbia followed few decades later, in 1835. The first Serbian constitution (Sretenjski ustav) was adopted at the national assembly in <a href="/wiki/Kragujevac" title="Kragujevac">Kragujevac</a> on February 15, 1835.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Canada" title="Constitution of Canada">Constitution of Canada</a> came into force on July 1, 1867, as the British North America Act, an act of the British Parliament. Over a century later, the BNA Act was patriated to the Canadian Parliament and augmented with the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" title="Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Apart from the <i>Constitution Acts, 1867 to 1982</i>, Canada's constitution also has unwritten elements based in common law and convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principles_of_constitutional_design">Principles of constitutional design</span></h2>
<p>After tribal people first began to live in cities and establish nations, many of these functioned according to unwritten customs, while some developed autocratic, even tyrannical monarchs, who <a href="/wiki/Rule_by_decree" title="Rule by decree">ruled by decree</a>, or mere personal whim. Such rule led some thinkers to take the position that what mattered was not the design of governmental institutions and operations, as much as the character of the rulers. This view can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, who called for rule by "philosopher-kings."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Later writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, would examine designs for government from a legal and historical standpoint.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> brought a series of political philosophers who wrote implied criticisms of the practices of monarchs and sought to identify principles of constitutional design that would be likely to yield more effective and just governance from their viewpoints. This began with revival of the Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_nations" title="Law of nations">law of nations</a> concept<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and its application to the relations among nations, and they sought to establish customary "laws of war and peace"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> to ameliorate wars and make them less likely. This led to considerations of what authority monarchs or other officials have and don't have, from where that authority derives, and the remedies for the abuse of such authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>A seminal juncture in this line of discourse arose in England from the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">Civil War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Cromwellian</a> <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Protectorate</a>, the writings of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Rutherford" title="Samuel Rutherford">Samuel Rutherford</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levellers" title="Levellers">Levellers</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Harrington_(author)" title="James Harrington (author)">James Harrington</a>, leading to the debate between <a href="/wiki/Robert_Filmer" title="Robert Filmer">Robert Filmer</a>, arguing for the divine right of monarchs, on the one side, and on the other, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Neville_(writer)" title="Henry Neville (writer)">Henry Neville</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Tyrrell_(writer)" title="James Tyrrell (writer)">James Tyrrell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Sidney" title="Algernon Sidney">Algernon Sidney</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>. What arose from the latter was a concept of government being erected on the foundations of first, a state of nature governed by natural laws, then a state of society, established by a social contract or compact, which bring underlying natural or social laws, before governments are formally established on them as foundations.
</p><p>Along the way several writers examined how the design of government was important, even if the government were headed by a monarch. They also classified various historical examples of governmental designs, typically into democracies, aristocracies, or monarchies, and considered how just and effective each tended to be and why, and how the advantages of each might be obtained by combining elements of each into a more complex design that balanced competing tendencies. Some, such as <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, also examined how the functions of government, such as legislative, executive, and judicial, might appropriately be separated into branches. The prevailing theme among these writers was that the design of constitutions is not completely arbitrary or a matter of taste. They generally held that there are underlying principles of design that constrain all constitutions for every polity or organization. Each built on the ideas of those before concerning what those principles might be.
</p><p>The later writings of <a href="/wiki/Orestes_Brownson" title="Orestes Brownson">Orestes Brownson</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> would try to explain what constitutional designers were trying to do. According to Brownson there are, in a sense, three "constitutions" involved: The first the <i>constitution of nature</i> that includes all of what was called "natural law." The second is the <i>constitution of society</i>, an unwritten and commonly understood set of rules for the society formed by a social contract before it establishes a government, by which it establishes the third, a <i>constitution of government</i>. The second would include such elements as the making of decisions by public <a href="/wiki/Convention_(meeting)" title="Convention (meeting)">conventions</a> called by <a href="/wiki/Public_notice" title="Public notice">public notice</a> and conducted by established <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_procedure" title="Parliamentary procedure">rules of procedure</a>. Each constitution must be consistent with, and derive its authority from, the ones before it, as well as from a historical act of society formation or constitutional ratification. Brownson argued that a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> is a society with effective dominion over a well-defined territory, that consent to a well-designed constitution of government arises from presence on that territory, and that it is possible for provisions of a written constitution of government to be "unconstitutional" if they are inconsistent with the constitutions of nature or society. Brownson argued that it is not ratification alone that makes a written constitution of government legitimate, but that it must also be competently designed and applied.
</p><p>Other writers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> have argued that such considerations apply not only to all national constitutions of government, but also to the constitutions of private organizations, that it is not an accident that the constitutions that tend to satisfy their members contain certain elements, as a minimum, or that their provisions tend to become very similar as they are amended after experience with their use. Provisions that give rise to certain kinds of questions are seen to need additional provisions for how to resolve those questions, and provisions that offer no course of action may best be omitted and left to policy decisions. Provisions that conflict with what Brownson and others can discern are the underlying "constitutions" of nature and society tend to be difficult or impossible to execute, or to lead to unresolvable disputes.
</p><p>Constitutional design has been treated as a kind of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metagame" title="Metagame">metagame</a> in which play consists of finding the best design and provisions for a written constitution that will be the rules for the game of government, and that will be most likely to optimize a balance of the utilities of justice, liberty, and security. An example is the metagame <a href="/wiki/Nomic" title="Nomic">Nomic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Political economy theory regards constitutions as coordination devices that help citizens to prevent rulers from abusing power. If the citizenry can coordinate a response to police government officials in the face of a constitutional fault, then the government have the incentives to honor the rights that the constitution guarantees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> An alternative view considers that constitutions are not enforced by the citizens at-large, but rather by the administrative powers of the state. Because rulers cannot themselves implement their policies, they need to rely on a set of organizations (armies, courts, police agencies, tax collectors) to implement it. In this position, they can directly sanction the government by refusing to cooperate, disabling the authority of the rulers. Therefore, constitutions could be characterized by a self-enforcing equilibria between the rulers and powerful administrators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_features">Key features</span></h2>
