</div>
</div><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="397" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg/170px-Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg/255px-Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg/340px-Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_copy_of_the_Russian_constitution.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/President_of_Russia" title="President of Russia">Presidential</a> copy of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Russia" title="Constitution of Russia">Russian Constitution</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Most commonly, the term <i>constitution</i> refers to a set of rules and principles that define the nature and extent of government. Most constitutions seek to regulate the relationship between institutions of the state, in a basic sense the relationship between the executive, legislature and the judiciary, but also the relationship of institutions within those branches. For example, executive branches can be divided into a head of government, government departments/ministries, executive agencies and a <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a>/administration. Most constitutions also attempt to define the relationship between individuals and the state, and to establish the broad rights of individual citizens. It is thus the most basic law of a territory from which all the other laws and rules are hierarchically derived; in some territories it is in fact called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_Law" title="Basic Law">Basic Law</a>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_2">Classification</span></h4>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Type</th>
<th>Form</th>
<th>Example
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Codified</td>
<td>In single act (document)</td>
<td>Most of the world (first: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">United States</a>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2">Uncodified</td>
<td>Fully written (in few documents)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_San_Marino" title="Constitution of San Marino">San Marino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basic_Laws_of_Israel" title="Basic Laws of Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basic_Law_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Basic Law of Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Partially unwritten (see <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">constitutional convention</a>)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_constitutional_documents" title="List of Canadian constitutional documents">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_New_Zealand" title="Constitution of New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Constitution of the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Codification">Codification</span></h4>
<p>A fundamental classification is codification or lack of codification. A codified constitution is one that is contained in a single document, which is the single source of constitutional law in a state. An uncodified constitution is one that is not contained in a single document, consisting of several different sources, which may be written or unwritten; see <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">constitutional convention</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Codified_constitution">Codified constitution</span></h5>
<p>Most states in the world have codified constitutions.
</p><p>Codified constitutions are often the product of some dramatic political change, such as a <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>. The process by which a country adopts a constitution is closely tied to the historical and political context driving this fundamental change. The legitimacy (and often the longevity) of codified constitutions has often been tied to the process by which they are initially adopted and some scholars have pointed out that high constitutional <a href="/wiki/Wiki-constitutionalism" title="Wiki-constitutionalism">turnover</a> within a given country may itself be detrimental to separation of powers and the rule of law.
</p><p>States that have codified constitutions normally give the constitution supremacy over ordinary <a href="/wiki/Statute" title="Statute">statute</a> law. That is, if there is any conflict between a legal statute and the codified constitution, all or part of the statute can be declared <i>ultra vires</i> by a court, and struck down as <a href="/wiki/Constitutionality" title="Constitutionality">unconstitutional</a>. In addition, exceptional procedures are often required to <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_amendment" title="Constitutional amendment">amend a constitution</a>. These procedures may include: convocation of a special <a href="/wiki/Constituent_assembly" title="Constituent assembly">constituent assembly</a> or constitutional convention, requiring a <a href="/wiki/Supermajority" title="Supermajority">supermajority</a> of legislators' votes, approval in two terms of <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliament</a>, the consent of regional legislatures, a <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> process, and/or other procedures that make amending a constitution more difficult than passing a simple law.
</p><p>Constitutions may also provide that their <a href="/wiki/Entrenched_clause" title="Entrenched clause">most basic principles can never be abolished, even by amendment</a>. In case a formally valid amendment of a constitution infringes these principles protected against any amendment, it may constitute a so-called <i>unconstitutional constitutional law</i>.
</p><p>Codified constitutions normally consist of a ceremonial <a href="/wiki/Preamble" title="Preamble">preamble</a>, which sets forth the goals of the state and the motivation for the constitution, and several articles containing the substantive provisions. The preamble, which is omitted in some constitutions, may contain a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_references_to_God" title="Constitutional references to God">reference to God</a> and/or to fundamental values of the state such as <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>. In ethnic nation-states such as <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, the mission of the state can be defined as preserving a specific nation, language and culture.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Uncodified_constitution">Uncodified constitution</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uncodified_constitution" title="Uncodified constitution">Uncodified constitution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magna_Carta_(British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4800" data-file-width="7200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg/220px-Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg/330px-Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg/440px-Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magna_Carta_(British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Constitution&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> only two sovereign states, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, have wholly uncodified constitutions. The <a href="/wiki/Basic_Laws_of_Israel" title="Basic Laws of Israel">Basic Laws of Israel</a> have since 1950 been intended to be the basis for a constitution, but as of 2017 it had not been drafted. The various Laws are considered to have precedence over other laws, and give the procedure by which they can be amended, typically by a simple majority of members of the Knesset (parliament).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lawsintro_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-lawsintro-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uncodified constitutions are the product of an "evolution" of laws and conventions over centuries (such as in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westminster_System" title="Westminster System">Westminster System</a> that developed in Britain). By contrast to codified constitutions, uncodified constitutions include both written sources – e.g. constitutional statutes enacted by the Parliament – and unwritten sources – <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">constitutional conventions</a>, observation of <a href="/wiki/Precedent" title="Precedent">precedents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention (norm)">customs</a> and traditions, such as holding general elections on Thursdays; together these constitute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_constitutional_law" title="British constitutional law">British constitutional law</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_constitutions">Mixed constitutions</span></h5>
<p>Some constitutions are largely, but not wholly, codified. For example, in the Constitution of Australia, most of its fundamental political principles and regulations concerning the relationship between branches of government, and concerning the government and the individual are codified in a single document, the Constitution of the Commonwealth of Australia. However, the presence of statutes with constitutional significance, namely the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_1931" title="Statute of Westminster 1931">Statute of Westminster</a>, as adopted by the Commonwealth in the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_Westminster_Adoption_Act_1942" title="Statute of Westminster Adoption Act 1942">Statute of Westminster Adoption Act 1942</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Australia_Act_1986" title="Australia Act 1986">Australia Act 1986</a> means that Australia's constitution is not contained in a single constitutional document.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It means the Constitution of Australia is uncodified,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (July 2020)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Constitution#Dubious" title="Talk:Constitution">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> it also contains <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">constitutional conventions</a>, thus is partially unwritten.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Canada" title="Constitution of Canada">Constitution of Canada</a> resulted from the passage of several <a href="/wiki/British_North_America_Acts" title="British North America Acts">British North America Acts</a> from 1867 to the <a href="/wiki/Canada_Act_1982" title="Canada Act 1982">Canada Act 1982</a>, the act that formally severed British Parliament's ability to amend the Canadian constitution. The Canadian constitution includes specific legislative acts as mentioned in section 52(2) of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act,_1982" title="Constitution Act, 1982">Constitution Act, 1982</a>. However, some documents not explicitly listed in section 52(2) are also considered constitutional documents in Canada, entrenched via reference; such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_1763" title="Proclamation of 1763">Proclamation of 1763</a>. Although Canada's constitution includes <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_constitutional_documents" title="List of Canadian constitutional documents">a number of different statutes, amendments, and references</a>, some constitutional rules that exist in Canada is derived from unwritten sources and constitutional conventions.
</p><p>The terms <i>written constitution</i> and <i>codified constitution</i> are often used interchangeably, as are <i>unwritten constitution</i> and <i>uncodified constitution</i>, although this usage is technically inaccurate. A codified constitution is a single document; states that do not have such a document have uncodified, but not entirely unwritten, constitutions, since much of an uncodified constitution is usually written in laws such as the <a href="/wiki/Basic_Laws_of_Israel" title="Basic Laws of Israel">Basic Laws of Israel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Acts_1911_and_1949" title="Parliament Acts 1911 and 1949">Parliament Acts</a> of the United Kingdom. Uncodified constitutions largely lack protection against amendment by the government of the time. For example, the U.K. <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_Parliaments_Act_2011" title="Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011">Fixed-term Parliaments Act 2011</a> legislated by simple majority for strictly <a href="/wiki/Fixed-term_election" title="Fixed-term election">fixed-term parliaments</a>; until then the ruling party could call a general election at any convenient time up to the maximum term of five years. This change would require a constitutional amendment in most nations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amendments">Amendments</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_amendment" title="Constitutional amendment">Constitutional amendment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constitution_of_the_United_States,_page_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8785" data-file-width="7258" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg/220px-Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg/330px-Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg/440px-Constitution_of_the_United_States%2C_page_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Constitution_of_the_United_States,_page_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a></div></div></div>
<p>A constitutional amendment is a modification of the constitution of a <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organization" title="Organization">organization</a> or other type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_entity" title="Legal entity">entity</a>. Amendments are often interwoven into the relevant sections of an existing constitution, directly altering the text. Conversely, they can be appended to the constitution as supplemental additions (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/codicil" title="wikt:codicil">codicils</a>), thus changing the frame of government without altering the existing text of the document.
</p><p>Most constitutions require that amendments cannot be enacted unless they have passed a special procedure that is more stringent than that required of ordinary legislation.
</p>

</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Entrenched_clauses">Entrenched clauses</span></h4>
<p>An entrenched clause or entrenchment clause of a <a href="/wiki/Basic_law" title="Basic law">basic law</a> or constitution is a provision that makes certain amendments either more difficult or impossible to pass, making such amendments inadmissible. Overriding an entrenched clause may require a <a href="/wiki/Supermajority" title="Supermajority">supermajority</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a>, or the consent of the minority party. For example, the U.S. Constitution has an entrenched clause that <a href="/wiki/Article_Five_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Constitutional_clauses_shielded_from_amendment" title="Article Five of the United States Constitution">prohibits abolishing equal suffrage of the States within the Senate without their consent</a>. The term eternity clause is used in a similar manner in the constitutions of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Czech_Republic" title="Constitution of the Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CECEE_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-CECEE-67">[67]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Germany" title="Constitution of Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Turkey" title="Constitution of Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Greece" title="Constitution of Greece">Greece</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Italy" title="Constitution of Italy">Italy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weakcon_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-weakcon-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Morocco" title="Constitution of Morocco">Morocco</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWC_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWC-70">[70]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Islamic_Republic_of_Iran" title="Constitution of the Islamic Republic of Iran">the Islamic Republic of Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Brazil" title="Constitution of Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">Norway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weakcon_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-weakcon-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">India</a> doesn't contain specific provisions on entrenched clauses but the <a href="/wiki/Basic_structure_doctrine" title="Basic structure doctrine">basic structure doctrine</a> makes it impossible for certain basic features of the Constitution to be altered or destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_India" title="Parliament of India">Parliament of India</a> through an <a href="/wiki/Amendment_of_the_Constitution_of_India" title="Amendment of the Constitution of India">amendment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Colombia" title="Constitution of Colombia">Colombia</a> also doesn't have explicit entrenched clauses but has similarly put a substantive limit on amending fundamental principles of their constitution through judicial interpretations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weakcon_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-weakcon-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_rights_and_duties">Constitutional rights and duties</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_right" title="Constitutional right">Constitutional right</a></div>
<p>Constitutions include various rights and duties. These include the following:
</p>
<ul><li>Duty to pay <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">Duty to serve in the military</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>Duty to work<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">Right to vote</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_assembly" title="Freedom of assembly">Freedom of assembly</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">Freedom of association</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_of_powers">Separation of powers</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">Separation of powers</a></div>
<p>Constitutions usually explicitly divide power between various branches of government. The standard model, described by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_de_Secondat,_baron_de_Montesquieu" title="Charles de Secondat, baron de Montesquieu">Baron de Montesquieu</a>, involves three branches of government: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_branch" title="Executive branch">executive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judicial</a>. Some constitutions include additional branches, such as an <a href="/wiki/Audit" title="Audit">auditory branch</a>. Constitutions vary extensively as to the degree of separation of powers between these branches.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Accountability">Accountability</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-presidential" title="Semi-presidential">semi-presidential</a> systems of government, department secretaries/ministers are accountable to the <a href="/wiki/President_(government_title)" title="President (government title)">president</a>, who has patronage powers to appoint and dismiss ministers. The president is accountable to the people in an election.
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary" title="Parliamentary">parliamentary</a> systems, Cabinet Ministers are accountable to <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">Parliament</a>, but it is the <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">prime minister</a> who appoints and dismisses them. In the case of the United Kingdom and other countries with a monarchy, it is the monarch who appoints and dismisses ministers, on the advice of the prime minister. In turn the prime minister will resign if the government loses the confidence of the parliament (or a part of it). Confidence can be lost if the government loses a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vote_of_no_confidence" title="Vote of no confidence">vote of no confidence</a> or, depending on the country,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> loses a particularly important vote in parliament, such as vote on the budget. When a government loses confidence, it stays in office until a new government is formed; something which normally but not necessarily required the holding of a general election.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_independent_institutions">Other independent institutions</span></h4>
<p>Other independent institutions which some constitutions have set out include a <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central bank</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/List_of_anti-corruption_agencies" title="List of anti-corruption agencies">anti-corruption commission</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Election_commission" title="Election commission">electoral commission</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> a judicial oversight body,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_commission" title="Human rights commission">human rights commission</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> a media commission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Ombudsman" title="Ombudsman">ombudsman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truth_and_reconciliation_commission" title="Truth and reconciliation commission">truth and reconciliation commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_structure">Power structure</span></h3>
<p>Constitutions also establish where sovereignty is located in the state. There are three basic types of distribution of sovereignty according to the degree of centralisation of power: unitary, federal, and confederal. The distinction is not absolute.
</p><p>In a unitary state, sovereignty resides in the state itself, and the constitution determines this. The territory of the state may be divided into regions, but they are not sovereign and are subordinate to the state. In the UK, the constitutional doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">Parliamentary sovereignty</a> dictates that sovereignty is ultimately contained at the centre. Some powers have been <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> (but not <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>). Some unitary states (<a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> is an example) devolve more and more power to sub-national governments until the state functions in practice much like a federal state.
</p><p>A federal state has a central structure with at most a small amount of territory mainly containing the institutions of the federal government, and several regions (called <i>states</i>, <i>provinces</i>, etc.) which compose the territory of the whole state. Sovereignty is divided between the centre and the constituent regions. The constitutions of Canada and the United States establish federal states, with power divided between the federal government and the provinces or states. Each of the regions may in turn have its own constitution (of unitary nature).
</p><p>A confederal state comprises again several regions, but the central structure has only limited coordinating power, and sovereignty is located in the regions. Confederal constitutions are rare, and there is often dispute to whether so-called "confederal" states are actually federal.
</p><p>To some extent a group of states which do not constitute a federation as such may by <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaties</a> and accords give up parts of their sovereignty to a <a href="/wiki/Supranational_union" title="Supranational union">supranational</a> entity. For example, the countries constituting the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> have agreed to abide by some Union-wide measures which restrict their absolute sovereignty in some ways, e.g., the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_system_of_measurement" title="Metric system of measurement">metric system of measurement</a> instead of national units previously used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_of_emergency">State of emergency</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">State of emergency</a></div>
<p>Many constitutions allow the declaration under exceptional circumstances of some form of state of emergency during which some rights and guarantees are suspended. This provision can be and has been abused to allow a government to suppress dissent without regard for human rights – see the article on <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Facade_constitutions">Facade constitutions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Constitutionalism" title="Constitutionalism">Constitutionalism</a></div>
<p>Italian political theorist <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Sartori" title="Giovanni Sartori">Giovanni Sartori</a> noted the existence of national constitutions which are a facade for authoritarian sources of power. While such documents may express respect for <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> or establish an independent judiciary, they may be ignored when the government feels threatened, or never put into practice. An extreme example was the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Constitution of the Soviet Union">Constitution of the Soviet Union</a> that on paper supported <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_assembly" title="Freedom of assembly">freedom of assembly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>; however, citizens who transgressed unwritten limits were summarily <a href="/wiki/Political_prisoner" title="Political prisoner">imprisoned</a>. The example demonstrates that the protections and benefits of a constitution are ultimately provided not through its written terms but through deference by government and society to its principles. A constitution may change from being real to a facade and back again as democratic and autocratic governments succeed each other.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_courts">Constitutional courts</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_court" title="Constitutional court">Constitutional court</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constitutionality" title="Constitutionality">Constitutionality</a></div>
<p>Constitutions are often, but by no means always, protected by a legal body whose job it is to interpret those constitutions and, where applicable, declare void executive and legislative acts which infringe the constitution. In some countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, this function is carried out by a dedicated constitutional court which performs this (and only this) function. In other countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>, the ordinary courts may perform this function in addition to their other responsibilities. While elsewhere, like in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the concept of declaring an act to be unconstitutional does not exist.
</p><p>A constitutional violation is an action or legislative act that is judged by a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_court" title="Constitutional court">constitutional court</a> to be contrary to the constitution, that is, unconstitutional. An example of constitutional violation by the executive could be a public office holder who acts outside the powers granted to that office by a constitution. An example of constitutional violation by the legislature is an attempt to pass a law that would contradict the constitution, without first going through the proper <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_amendment" title="Constitutional amendment">constitutional amendment</a> process.
</p><p>Some countries, mainly those with uncodified constitutions, have no such courts at all. For example, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> has traditionally operated under the principle of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">parliamentary sovereignty</a> under which the laws passed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Parliament" title="United Kingdom Parliament">United Kingdom Parliament</a> could not be questioned by the courts.
</p>

Coase-Sandor Institute for Law &amp; Economics Working Paper No. 694. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SSRN_(identifier)" title="SSRN (identifier)">SSRN</a> <a class="external text" href="//papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2477530" rel="nofollow">2477530</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Coase-Sandor+Institute+for+Law+%26+Economics&amp;rft.atitle=Do+Constitutional+Rights+Make+a+Difference%3F&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fpapers.ssrn.com%2Fsol3%2Fpapers.cfm%3Fabstract_id%3D2477530%23id-name%3DSSRN&amp;rft.aulast=Chilton&amp;rft.aufirst=Adam+S.&amp;rft.au=Versteeg%2C+Mila&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AConstitution"></span>)</span>

<ul><li>Reuven Firestone, <i>Jihād: the origin of holy war in Islam</i> (1999) p. 118;</li>
<li>"Muhammad", <i>Encyclopedia of Islam Online</i></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Watt. Muhammad at Medina and R.B. Serjeant "The Constitution of Medina." <i>Islamic Quarterly</i> 8 (1964) p. 4.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">R.B. Serjeant, <i>The Sunnah Jami'ah, pacts with the Yathrib Jews, and the Tahrim of Yathrib: Analysis and translation of the documents comprised in the so-called "Constitution of Medina."</i> Bulletin of the School of Oriental and African Studies, University of London, Vol. 41, No. 1. (1978), p. 4.</span>

<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Watt. <i>Muhammad at Medina</i>. pp. 227–228 Watt argues that the initial agreement was shortly after the hijra and the document was amended at a later date specifically after the battle of Badr (AH [anno hijra] 2, = AD 624). Serjeant argues that the constitution is in fact 8 different treaties which can be dated according to events as they transpired in Medina with the first treaty being written shortly after Muhammad's arrival. R. B. Serjeant. "The Sunnah Jâmi'ah, Pacts with the Yathrib Jews, and the Tahrîm of Yathrib: Analysis and Translation of the Documents Comprised in the so called 'Constitution of Medina'." in <i>The Life of Muhammad: The Formation of the Classical Islamic World</i>: Volume iv. Ed. Uri Rubin. Brookfield: Ashgate, 1998, p. 151 and see same article in BSOAS 41 (1978): 18 ff. See also Caetani. <i>Annali dell'Islam, Volume I</i>. Milano: Hoepli, 1905, p. 393. Julius Wellhausen. <i>Skizzen und Vorabeiten</i>, IV, Berlin: Reimer, 1889, pp. 82ff who argue that the document is a single treaty agreed upon shortly after the hijra. Wellhausen argues that it belongs to the first year of Muhammad's residence in Medina, before the battle of Badr in 2/624. Wellhausen bases this judgement on three considerations; first Muhammad is very diffident about his own position, he accepts the Pagan tribes within the Umma, and maintains the Jewish clans as clients of the Ansars see Wellhausen, Excursus, p. 158. Even Moshe Gil a skeptic of Islamic history argues that it was written within 5 months of Muhammad's arrival in Medina. Moshe Gil. "The Constitution of Medina: A Reconsideration." <i>Israel Oriental Studies</i> 4 (1974): p. 45.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://research.kent.ac.uk/warandnation/1811-miranda-declares-independence-in-venezuela-and-civil-war-begins/" rel="nofollow">"1811 Miranda Declares Independence in Venezuela and Civil War Begins"</a>. <i>War and Nation: identity and the process of state-building in South America (1800-1840)</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">February 1,</span> 2020</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=War+and+Nation%3A+identity+and+the+process+of+state-building+in+South+America+%281800-1840%29&amp;rft.atitle=1811+Miranda+Declares+Independence+in+Venezuela+and+Civil+War+Begins&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fresearch.kent.ac.uk%2Fwarandnation%2F1811-miranda-declares-independence-in-venezuela-and-civil-war-begins%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AConstitution"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFPayne1973"><a href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Payne" title="Stanley G. Payne">Payne, Stanley G.</a> (1973). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historyofspainpo00payn/page/432" rel="nofollow"><i>A History of Spain and Portugal: Eighteenth Century to Franco</i></a>. Vol. 2. Madison: University of Wisconsin Press. pp. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historyofspainpo00payn/page/432" rel="nofollow">432–433</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-299-06270-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-299-06270-5"><bdi>978-0-299-06270-5</bdi></a>. <q>The Spanish pattern of conspiracy and revolt by liberal army officers ... was emulated in both Portugal and Italy. In the wake of Riego's successful rebellion, the first and only pronunciamiento in Italian history was carried out by liberal officers in the kingdom of the Two Sicilies. The Spanish-style military conspiracy also helped to inspire the beginning of the Russian revolutionary movement with the revolt of the <a href="/wiki/Decembrist_revolt" title="Decembrist revolt">Decembrist army officers</a> in 1825. Italian liberalism in 1820–1821 relied on junior officers and the provincial middle classes, essentially the same social base as in Spain. It even used a Hispanized political vocabulary, for it was led by <i>giunte</i> (juntas), appointed local <i>capi politici</i> (<i>jefes políticos</i>), used the terms of <i>liberali</i> and <i>servili</i> (emulating the Spanish word serviles applied to supporters of absolutism), and in the end talked of resisting by means of a <i>guerrilla</i>. For both Portuguese and Italian liberals of these years, the Spanish constitution of 1812 remained the standard document of reference.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+Spain+and+Portugal%3A+Eighteenth+Century+to+Franco&amp;rft.place=Madison&amp;rft.pages=432-433&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Wisconsin+Press&amp;rft.date=1973&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-299-06270-5&amp;rft.aulast=Payne&amp;rft.aufirst=Stanley+G.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fhistoryofspainpo00payn%2Fpage%2F432&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AConstitution"></span></span>
</li>

"Majoritarian and Consensus Democracy: the Swedish Experience". <i>Scandinavian Political Studies</i>. <b>21</b> (3): 195–206. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1467-9477.1998.tb00012.x" rel="nofollow">10.1111/j.1467-9477.1998.tb00012.x</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Scandinavian+Political+Studies&amp;rft.atitle=Majoritarian+and+Consensus+Democracy%3A+the+Swedish+Experience&amp;rft.volume=21&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=195-206&amp;rft.date=2007-05-01&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fj.1467-9477.1998.tb00012.x&amp;rft.aulast=Lewin&amp;rft.aufirst=Leif&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AConstitution"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.hellenicparliament.gr/UserFiles/f3c70a23-7696-49db-9148-f24dce6a27c8/001-156%20aggliko.pdf" rel="nofollow">The official English language translation of the Greek Constitution as of May 27, 2008</a>, Article 110 §1, p. 124, source: Hellenic Parliament, "The provisions of the Constitution shall be subject to revision with the exception of those which determine the form of government as a Parliamentary Republic and those of articles 2 paragraph 1, 4 paragraphs 1, 4 and 7 , 5 paragraphs 1 and 3, 13 paragraph 1, and 26."</span>
